201407	2629	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201407	2630	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	As President Plosser just mentioned and as others have remarked, the extent of the first-quarter contraction in real GDP is deeply puzzling in light of the wide array of data that point to solid gains in economic activity.	13	1
201407	2631	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	So my staff actually went to see how big this disconnect was between the GDP number—the decline of nearly 3 percent at an annual rate in the first quarter—and what we saw in the other data.	13	1
201407	2632	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	For this purpose they used a statistical FAVAR model that takes 121 individual economic indicators and looks for the common trend in those.	13	1
201407	2633	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And this FAVAR model that doesn’t use GDP would have predicted real GDP growth of 2¾ percent in the first quarter—almost exactly what was reported, except for the sign.	13	1
201407	2634	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	[Laughter]	13	1
201407	2635	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	So it is a striking statistical way to see that what we are saying, and what I think the Board staff said in the Tealbook, is really supported by the data.	13	1
201407	2636	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Pretty much everything that we threw in the hopper was telling us that we were continuing with moderate growth.	13	1
201407	2637	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I wouldn’t declare the entire shortfall in first-quarter growth to be a measurement error, but I do agree with the Tealbook and many others that it does appear that the first-quarter GDP number is an anomaly.	13	1
201407	2638	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	We shouldn’t take much signal from it in terms of the underlying pace of expansion.	13	1
201407	2639	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And overall, the broad set of incoming data is consistent with moderate growth for the rest of the year, as well as gradual progress toward our inflation goal.	13	1

201407	2642	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	One area that continues to surprise on the upside is the labor market.	13	1
201407	2643	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Again, I think we have seen a wide range of indicators in the labor market showing improvement as good as or better than we were expecting.	13	1
201407	2644	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Notably, payroll and household employment have been showing solid gains.	13	1
201407	2645	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Many of us have already referred to this good news concerning the labor market, but the one striking exception is the tepid pace of compensation growth, which, despite the improvement in the labor market, continues to run around 2 percent or so.	13	1
201407	2646	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And this is obviously an important issue, because we look at wage growth both as a sign of the state of the labor market in terms of slack—in terms of our maximum employment mandate—and also as a signal of its effects on inflationary pressures, in terms of our inflation mandate.	13	1
201407	2647	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	A couple of meetings ago I talked about some of the research at the San Francisco Fed about the effects of downward nominal wage rigidities that may be influencing the pattern of compensation growth and holding it back over the past couple of years.	13	1
201407	2648	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	But still, there is a deeper puzzle about what has been happening with wages for several years, and I am going to focus the rest of my comments on some of the longer-term structural changes in the labor market, specifically concerning labor’s share of income, which obviously affects compensation growth and also inflationary pressures.	13	1
201407	2649	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s good to remember that in many economic models, including FRB/US and most DSGE models, labor share is a key driver of marginal cost and inflationary pressures.	13	1
201407	2650	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Over the past few years, labor share in the nonfarm business sector has averaged about 57 percent, which is well below its long-run historical average of around 62 percent.	13	1
201407	2651	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	If you take it at face value, which most models would do, this metric—this very low labor share—would suggest very low cost pressures for inflation.	13	1
201407	2652	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	It suggests also that wage growth should be quite strong in the period ahead.	13	1
201407	2653	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	However, my message is one of caution against relying on where the labor share is relative to some historical measure of its normal value in gauging either labor market slack or inflationary pressures.	13	1
201407	2654	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	One of the things that many of us were taught is that the labor share in the United States is relatively constant.	13	1
201407	2655	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And that was true for several decades.	13	1
201407	2656	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	But in fact, when you do a deeper dive, looking underneath the fact that labor share is relatively constant for the first four decades after World War II, there were actually a lot of things happening.	13	1
201407	2657	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	In certain sectors it was going up, and in others it was going down.	13	1
201407	2658	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	So in a way it was pure coincidence that it happened to seem so flat.	13	1
201407	2659	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Actually, since the late 1980s, we have seen a substantial downward trend in labor share.	13	1
201407	2660	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And the fact that it has been going on for so long—it’s not related to the current recession or recovery—suggests that there really are structural, rather than cyclical, factors driving this, or at least most of this.	13	1
201407	2661	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	There is quite a bit of literature on this now, talking about what could be causing labor share to decline.	13	1
201407	2662	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Maybe it’s information technology capital replacing routine labor, the effects of globalization, or the reduced bargaining power of workers.	13	1
201407	2663	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Due to the timing of the decline, one of the prime suspects has been the increasing rate at which IT capital is replacing labor.	13	1
201407	2664	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	If robots replace workers on the auto assembly line, a greater share of the price of a car may go to capital rather than labor.	13	1
201407	2665	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Another explanation involves globalization:  If the labor-intensive jobs, such as those at call centers, are offshored, the income share of the US economy going to domestic workers is reduced.	13	1
201407	2666	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Of course, these explanations for a lower labor share are likely complementary and reinforcing, and I am not going to take a stand on the question, is it IT, is it labor bargaining power, or is it globalization.	13	1
201407	2667	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	But the key theme that comes out of this literature is that these are long-running structural changes in our labor market.	13	1
201407	2668	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	So in our own thinking about where the economy is going, we shouldn’t be expecting labor share to go back to some normal historical level.	13	1
201407	2669	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	In fact, looking forward, it is really hard to know which way labor share should go.	13	1
201407	2670	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Should it stay at its very low level of around 57 percent?	13	1
201407	2671	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Should it rise somewhat as the economy improves?	13	1
201407	2672	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Or maybe this downward trend will actually continue into the future.	13	1
201407	2673	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	So I view labor share, which used to be viewed as one of these cosmological constants— these great stylized facts of macroeconomics, like potential output growth, like the natural rate of interest, and a lot of things that we took for granted were constant, probably never really were, and clearly aren’t today.	13	1
201407	2674	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And so in thinking about the implications of that, I think what it means is that we shouldn’t be expecting labor share to be moving back.	13	1
201407	2675	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	We should be more realistic about our ability to measure the equilibrium value of wages, or real wages, relative to productivity.	13	1
201407	2676	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Looking forward, if we thought the labor share would return to its earlier postwar benchmark, we would be expecting strong compensation growth relative to productivity growth.	13	1
201407	2677	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	But my reading of this literature is that a large part of this decline is really driven by structural factors, and a full recovery in the labor market may have real wages growing at no more than the pace of potential productivity growth, and perhaps actually below that, because if you have a declining labor share, you would actually expect real wages to be growing slightly slower than productivity growth.	13	1
201407	2678	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	So I think it’s one of the factors that can help us understand why wage growth hasn’t been as strong as we might have thought.	13	1
201407	2679	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	I still think there is a significant cyclical aspect of this.	13	1
201407	2680	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’m not downplaying that.	13	1
201407	2681	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	But we have been going through all of the factors that might explain why wage growth has been well below what we thought.	13	1
201407	2682	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	And I think we just have to keep this in mind when we think about what the new normal for the economy is.	13	1
201407	2683	2014-07-30	meeting	WILLIAMS	Pretty much every variable seems to have a new normal, and we need to adjust this in our models and our thinking.	13	1

201407	2724	2014-07-30	meeting	FISHER	From the standpoint of the issue of wage/price pressure, there are certain areas where you see it, certainly in an area of robust economic growth like we have, or like you have in San Francisco and the Bay Area.	13	1

201407	2762	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	This is something of a new development.	13	1

201407	2766	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Most companies still report merit increases of 2 to 3 percent, but we heard more mention of higher increases either planned or already implemented.	13	1

201407	2768	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	We are beginning to hear claims of successes in pushing through price increases.	13	1
201407	2769	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Several companies said they are intending to increase prices over the remainder of the year and are confident the price increases will stick.	13	1
201407	2770	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Because the estimation of labor resource slack is so central to policy questions, my staff did further work over the intermeeting period trying to more completely understand the total part-time worker phenomenon.	13	1
201407	2771	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Based on data in the Census Bureau’s Current Population Survey, the total part-time share of employment rose about 3 percentage points to 19.7 percent during the recession, and it is currently about 2 percentage points higher than before the recession, at 18.3 percent.	13	1
201407	2772	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Almost all of the increase in the part-time employment share is accounted for by the rise in involuntary part-time workers.	13	1
201407	2773	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	The rise in PTER is broad based and can be seen in almost every industry and occupational group.	13	1
201407	2774	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	The greatest increases in PTER occurred in industries that typically have a large part-time workforce—retail sales, food services, personal care, health- care support, and construction.	13	1
201407	2775	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	But to repeat, PTER increased across a wide spectrum of industries and occupations.	13	1
201407	2776	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	My staff looked into whether feedback from operating businesses in our District lines up with the thesis that the elevated level of people working part time reflects primarily cyclical as opposed to structural forces.	13	1
201407	2777	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Additionally, my staff examined whether the weakness we have seen in hourly wage growth is consistent or uniform across both full-time and part-time workers.	13	1
201407	2778	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	To get at the first question, we conducted a survey involving a good spread of about 400 midsize to large firms in our District and asked them to compare their utilization of part-time workers today versus before the recession.	13	1
201407	2779	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	We also asked them to cite the main reason for any change in their mix of full-time and part-time workers.	13	1
201407	2780	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	We found that 25 percent of firms have a higher share of part-time workers.	13	1
201407	2781	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Those citing weak business conditions as the reason for greater use of part-time workers were about equal with those citing the increasing hourly compensation costs of full-time workers associated with the Affordable Care Act, for example.	13	1
201407	2782	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Our survey results suggest that there is a combination of cyclical and structural explanations for the rise in part-time workers.	13	1
201407	2783	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	On the wage growth question, we found from data in the CPS that the growth rate of median hourly wages for people who are working part time for economic reasons has been flat since 2007, while wages have increased about 5 percent over this period for voluntary part-time workers and 14½ percent for full-time workers.	13	1
201407	2784	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Our tentative conclusion is the weakness in aggregate hourly wage growth seems to have been influenced by relatively stagnant wages of part-time workers in general, but especially PTER workers.	13	1
201407	2785	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Turning to my outlook, my current thinking is very similar to that outlined in the Tealbook.	13	1
201407	2786	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	I expect the second-quarter bounceback to be less impressive than earlier expected, but I am holding to the view that the growth run rate is accelerating to around 3 percent.	13	1
201407	2787	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Looking at indicators of GDP growth, I see the data on payroll employment, industrial production and manufacturing, and business capital investment, as indicated by capital goods shipments, all supporting the assumption of something near a 3 percent run rate.	13	1
201407	2788	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	The consumer spending trend is less affirming of a 3 percent assumption.	13	1
201407	2789	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	So I take that as a reason for reserving judgment on the true strength and momentum of the economy pending more validation by incoming data.	13	1
201407	2790	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	As regards the balance of risks, I continue to see the risks to the growth and inflation outlooks as broadly balanced.	13	1

201407	2793	2014-07-30	meeting	LOCKHART	Since I am in the camp of putting substantial weight on other labor market indicators, the risks around U-3 per se influence my thinking on appropriate policy less than growth risks and inflation risks.	13	1

201407	2842	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	District payroll employment has increased at nearly a 1 percent annual pace so far this year, very similar to the nation and strong enough to have driven the Fourth District’s unemployment rate down to 5.9 percent, slightly below the national average.	13	1
201407	2843	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Over the longer run, District employment growth has tended to be less than the US average, and unemployment has tended to be higher, so I read the labor market trends in the District as being favorable.	13	1
201407	2844	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Anecdotal reports continue to suggest that more employment gains are likely in the future, with a large proportion of contacts reporting planned increases in staffing levels, although at a modest pace.	13	1
201407	2845	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	In addition, in light of improving conditions, about half of the contacts we surveyed for this meeting indicated they intend to increase capital expenditures in coming months.	13	1
201407	2846	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	This is a significantly higher fraction than at the end of last year.	13	1

201407	2848	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	While residential building activity appears to have changed little in recent months, nonresidential building activity is picking up.	13	1
201407	2849	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	So far, there are few signs of price pressures either building or diminishing among firms in the District.	13	1
201407	2850	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	So the bottom line is that the Fourth District’s economy is making steady progress and the outlook is for that to continue.	13	1
201407	2851	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	At the national level, like the Tealbook, I attribute most of the first-quarter weakness to transitory factors, especially the bad weather.	13	1
201407	2852	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	I take more signal about the underlying strength of the economy from the improvement in labor market conditions, which has exceeded the expectations of many economists.	13	1
201407	2853	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	The unemployment rate has fallen to 6.1 percent.	13	1
201407	2854	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	That’s nearly 1 percentage point lower than last July’s Tealbook was projecting it to be at this point.	13	1
201407	2855	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	While long-term unemployment remains elevated, the declines over the past year in a broad set of unemployment rate measures are some of the largest we have seen in many years.	13	1
201407	2856	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Payroll employment growth has also strengthened and is running above expectations.	13	1
201407	2857	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	On the inflation front, the data continue to be consistent with inflation gradually picking up to our 2 percent goal over time.	13	1
201407	2858	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	The Cleveland Fed’s median and trimmed mean CPI measures decelerated last month, as did the overall CPI.	13	1
201407	2859	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Yet even with the most recent readings, by a number of measures including the CPI, core CPI, and the Cleveland Fed’s median CPI and trimmed mean CPI, year-over-year inflation has increased since the start of the year.	13	1
201407	2860	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	My staff’s VAR model forecasts inflation to be on a positive trajectory over the forecast horizon.	13	1
201407	2861	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Rising inflation is consistent with a pickup in economic growth and inflation expectations well anchored at our target.	13	1
201407	2862	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	And I note that the Cleveland Fed’s 10-year inflation expectations measure has remained essentially stable near 2 percent.	13	1
201407	2863	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	One question is whether this is a reasonable inflation forecast, as we haven’t seen much acceleration in wages yet.	13	1
201407	2864	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	So I asked my staff to investigate the lead-lag structure of wage and price inflation.	13	1
201407	2865	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Their analysis included several measures of wages and broader compensation and concluded that it has been difficult to find a lead-lag relationship between wages and prices.	13	1
201407	2866	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	The strongest correlations are contemporaneous ones, especially since the mid-1980s.	13	1
201407	2867	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	More-formal Granger causality tests are also quite mixed and, if anything, lean toward suggesting that price inflation Granger-causes wage inflation.	13	1
201407	2868	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Moreover, my staff finds no evidence that wages and compensation help to predict price inflation out of sample.	13	1
201407	2869	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Similar to a finding reported in the Tealbook, my staff found there hasn’t been a stable Phillips-curve relationship between unemployment gaps and price inflation since the 1980s, but there does seem to be a more stable relationship for wage inflation.	13	1
201407	2870	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Taken together, these results suggest that we should continue to include wage developments as an indicator when assessing tightness in labor markets, but that current subdued wage growth is not inconsistent with our forecasts of rising price inflation.	13	1
201407	2871	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	We should expect wages to rise with prices, not necessarily to lead prices.	13	1
201407	2872	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	In addition, we cannot simply wait for wages to accelerate before assessing the implications of future price inflation for the stance of monetary policy.	13	1
201407	2873	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	So I continue to forecast that inflation will move back toward target over time.	13	1
201407	2874	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Similarly, I have made little change in my growth and employment forecast since last time.	13	1
201407	2875	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	I project that growth will be about 3 percent in the second half of this year through 2016, and that the unemployment rate will continue to decline.	13	1
201407	2876	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	I see the risks around my forecast, including international developments, as broadly balanced.	13	1
201407	2877	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	At this point, financial-stability risk is low, but leveraged lending is something that we need to continue to monitor carefully.	13	1
201407	2878	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Now, my projections incorporate continued reductions in the pace of purchases, with the program ending in October.	13	1
201407	2879	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	With continued steady progress in labor markets toward the Committee’s longer-run goal of maximum employment, I tie liftoff from the zero lower bound to when inflation between one and two years ahead reaches the Committee’s goal of 2 percent.	13	1
201407	2880	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	After liftoff, my funds rate path follows the path suggested by a Taylor rule with inertia.	13	1
201407	2881	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	In my projection, liftoff occurs in the first quarter of 2015.	13	1
201407	2882	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Using my liftoff criterion, this is sooner than would have been projected at the start of this year, reflecting better-than-expected realized and projected progress toward our dual-mandate goals.	13	1
201407	2883	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	We have been consistently underestimating the economic progress being made.	13	1
201407	2884	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	The Tealbook sees the output gap as 1½ percentage points narrower than last July’s Tealbook was estimating it to be at this point.	13	1
201407	2885	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	The current Tealbook now sees the output gap being eliminated by next summer.	13	1
201407	2886	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	This is three quarters earlier than in the June Tealbook.	13	1
201407	2887	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	I think it is prudent to plan for the possibility of liftoff sooner than market participants are currently anticipating.	13	1
201407	2888	2014-07-30	meeting	MESTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201407	2900	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	Turning to the national economy, I continue to believe that we are in a wait-and-see mode, especially with respect to economic growth.	13	1
201407	2901	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	We need confirmation that the underlying strength that seems to be apparent in anecdotal reports on the economy as well as in labor market data is actually showing through in GDP performance.	13	1
201407	2902	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	But I largely agree with the Tealbook’s baseline interpretation that growth will be relatively strong in the second, third, and fourth quarters of 2014 and into 2016.	13	1
201407	2903	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	In conjunction with this scenario, I see unemployment continuing to fall, other labor market indicators continuing to show improvement, and inflation continuing to rise to eventually exceed our 2 percent inflation target in 2015.	13	1
201407	2904	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	I want to pause and acknowledge the box on multiple equilibriums in the Tealbook.	13	1
201407	2905	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	I’m an advocate of multiple-equilibrium stories, and I appreciated the appearance of this in the Tealbook.	13	1
201407	2906	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	I think the Tealbook gave a fair characterization that this continues to be a risk to any scenario—mine in particular, but anybody’s scenario about the US economy.	13	1
201407	2907	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	I have become more concerned that something of a mismatch is developing between the distance of macroeconomic outcomes from Committee goals, on the one hand, and the distance of policy tools from normal settings, on the other.	13	1
201407	2908	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	In short, we are quite close in terms of macroeconomic goals, but as distanced as we have been in the postwar era from normal in terms of policy tool settings.	13	1
201407	2909	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	In terms of macroeconomic goals, inflation is just a few ticks from target, and unemployment, at 6.1 percent, is only somewhat higher than many of the estimates of the long- run rate put forward by members of this Committee.	13	1
201407	2910	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	If one puts these numbers into a loss function based on squared deviations from long-run values with equal weight on unemployment and inflation—the type of loss function often used in staff assessments of optimal policy—one finds that current goal variables are quite close to target compared with historical experience since 1960.	13	1
201407	2911	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	In fact, we are closer to goals today than we have been most of the time historically.	13	1
201407	2912	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	Of all of the months since 1960, we’re doing better today by this criterion than we have approximately 80 percent of the time.	13	1
201407	2913	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	Policy tool settings, in contrast, are nowhere near normal.	13	1
201407	2914	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	The policy rate, at near zero, is 350 to 400 basis points below normal.	13	1
201407	2915	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	The size of the balance sheet was on the order of 5 to 6 percent of GDP before the crisis but today is on the order of 25 percent of GDP.	13	1
201407	2916	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	If one puts numbers like this into a similar quadratic objective based on squared deviations of the policy rate from normal and the balance sheet from normal with equal weight on the two dimensions, one finds that the current values are as far from normal as we have ever been in the postwar era since 1960.	13	1
201407	2917	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	These simple calculations indicate a growing mismatch between what monetary policy is doing and the reality on the ground for the US economy.	13	1
201407	2918	2014-07-30	meeting	BULLARD	If the economy confirms the relatively strong growth expectations that I have and that are contained in the Tealbook in the months and quarters ahead, this mismatch between policy settings and actual macroeconomic outcomes will become a more pressing issue for this Committee.	13	1

201407	2939	2014-07-30	meeting	GEORGE	My staff has done analysis showing job growth has been strong this year for workers with a college education entering high-skilled occupations, which has occurred alongside their declining employment in middle-skill occupations.	13	1

201407	2950	2014-07-30	meeting	GEORGE	While the improvements in the labor market are encouraging, there is still the question of wage growth, but even here, growth in average hourly earnings for production and nonsupervisory workers has moved higher since late 2012, and the number of firms reporting few or no qualified applicants for job openings in the NFIB survey is back to pre-crisis levels.	13	1

201407	3190	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Information reflected in surveys of business intentions to hire in the coming quarters, while not hard data, is still somewhat encouraging as well.	13	1
201407	3191	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	All of this, though, still leaves us with the questions of how much more slack remains and what all of this might mean for the future path of inflation.	13	1
201407	3192	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	With respect to the question of slack, in theory, obviously, this should be pretty straightforward.	13	1
201407	3193	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	We just look for evidence of general, as opposed to sectoral or regional, wage inflation, and we’ll know that there’s a decreasing amount of, and maybe very little, slack left in labor markets.	13	1
201407	3194	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	But, in practice, as we all know, that’s hard to do because compensation data don’t seem as reliable or as uniform as other labor market indicators.	13	1
201407	3195	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	There are different measures, which may not track one another too closely, particularly in the short-to-medium term.	13	1
201407	3196	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Having said that, I note that it still does not seem as though there’s any evidence of generalized wage increases, as several of you commented yesterday.	13	1
201407	3197	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Hourly earnings for production and nonsupervisory workers are up.	13	1
201407	3198	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Some have pointed to the household survey on earnings.	13	1
201407	3199	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	But if the issue is whether labor markets are tightening, pushing up costs that employers have to pay for workers, it seems as though we should be looking most at broader-based gauges of what those employers are reporting that they’re paying.	13	1
201407	3200	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	I think that’s why the ECI and the hourly compensation component of the Productivity and Costs survey are most relied on by the staff for purposes of evaluating trends in wages.	13	1
201407	3201	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	And, with respect to those two, neither, at least to date, shows signs of any pickup.	13	1
201407	3202	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	But it’s true that staff forecasts have been expecting that pickup for some time, and we do have more anecdotes and surveys suggesting a wider range of employers intending to raise wages as well as to do some more hiring.	13	1
201407	3203	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	So I think we’re still left with looking at some of these other indexes of labor market slack, the ones I referred to earlier.	13	1
201407	3204	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	As has been the case for a while, some of them can be interpreted either way, probably depending on one’s conceptual and policy priors.	13	1
201407	3205	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	The increase in job openings is the current example of that.	13	1
201407	3206	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	You can explain it either as a growing mismatch between skills and job availability or as the next step in a stronger labor market that will be followed by larger gross hiring numbers.	13	1
201407	3207	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	We just don’t know yet which of those will play out and reveal itself to be the correct explanation.	13	1
201407	3208	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	But there are the data streams that seem pretty good evidence of continued slack.	13	1
201407	3209	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	One such piece of evidence is the decline, I think, in the long-term unemployment rate in the first half of this year and, particularly, what that reveals about a movement of people into and out of the labor force and into work when one looks behind the numbers.	13	1
201407	3210	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Since February, the absolute number of the long-term unemployed has declined from 3.8 million to 3 million.	13	1
201407	3211	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	The share of the long-term unemployed in total unemployment is now down to under 33 percent from the high that was in the mid-40s immediately following the recession.	13	1
201407	3212	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	This is encouraging because, as I noted earlier, this has happened in the context of the labor force participation rate stabilizing and the EPOP moving up.	13	1
201407	3213	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	The transition rate from long-term unemployment to employment has been moving up in the past several months, but it’s also true that the transition rate from long-term unemployment to being out of the labor force has moved up in the past couple of months.	13	1
201407	3214	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	So that fact reminds us that, based on monthly transition rates out of long-term unemployment to either jobs or nonparticipation over the past several years, the number of the long-term unemployed should be much smaller than it actually is.	13	1
201407	3215	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Some research that was recently posted on the Board’s website by Tomaz Cajner and David Ratner of the Board’s staff suggests what the answer to that puzzle may be and, in the process of doing so, makes, again, some case for the continuation of a nontrivial amount of labor market slack.	13	1
201407	3216	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	They’ve hypothesized that some of the long-term unemployed may be moving from that status to nonparticipation—not actively looking for work—several times before they end up either employed or more or less permanently out of the workforce.	13	1
201407	3217	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	To develop that hypothesis, they also looked at annual, rather than monthly, data on longitudinally matched individuals and found that individuals who were classified as long-term unemployed a year ago have a 38 percent likelihood of being employed today.	13	1
201407	3218	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	That’s the highest level since 2008.	13	1
201407	3219	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	In fact, individuals classified as long-term unemployed a year ago are more likely to be employed today than they are to be out of the workforce.	13	1
201407	3220	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	So Tomaz and David suggested that a deeper dive into the data yielded a fairly encouraging picture consistent with the hypothesis that the long-term unemployed in this still-unprecedented postwar economic episode are, to a significant though not complete degree, relevant for thinking about labor market slack and thus for the impact of the long-term unemployed on wages in particular.	13	1
201407	3221	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	As to the question of what all of this means for inflation, I think, first, it suggests that we’ve got at least some distance to go before the labor market is truly tight, suggesting in turn the possibility that increases in wages may be more gradual than sudden as they do begin to occur.	13	1
201407	3222	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Second, it’s also worth remembering that levels of real wage and compensation increases over the past few years have been running below even the modestly recorded productivity gains that we have.	13	1
201407	3223	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	And, third and most important, the relationship between wage inflation and overall inflation is harder to pin down than we might think—I believe President Mester alluded to this yesterday—and that’s related to, as Governor Fischer mentioned, the flat Phillips curve over a fairly long period of time and the work that the Board’s staff did a couple of years ago suggesting that the pass-through of labor cost to core inflation has become progressively more attenuated in the quarter-century beginning in 1985.	13	1
201407	3224	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	It’s obviously possible that curves that have flattened, or relationships that have attenuated, over some period of time could revert to their previous state.	13	1
201407	3225	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	But it seems to me that we shouldn’t take as an article of faith that wage increases will translate fairly quickly into significant inflation increases.	13	1
201407	3226	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	It would be useful, at least to me, to understand why those holding this view think that enough has changed from that pattern of the past couple of decades to have the kind of phenomenon that I think people associate with big run-ups in inflation 30, 35, or 40 years ago.	13	1
201407	3227	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	If all of that is true, it leaves us in the somewhat uncomfortable position of perhaps not having an early indicator of where inflation may be going, as would be the case if wage inflation were a pretty good foreshadowing of what was going to happen with inflation overall.	13	1
201407	3228	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	But that’s just the situation in which we may find ourselves for the coming couple of quarters.	13	1
201407	3229	2014-07-30	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201407	3230	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201407	3231	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Powell.	13	1
201407	3232	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201407	3233	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	I would agree that the reduction in the estimated output gap in the Tealbook baseline forecast is particularly important and, potentially, quite significant.	13	1
201407	3234	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	It seems to me, therefore, that this is an important time to update prior views and consider the implications for policy.	13	1
201407	3235	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	I’ll start with inflation.	13	1
201407	3236	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Since I joined the Committee in May 2012, the risk has really been on the downside.	13	1
201407	3237	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Regarding inflation for 2013, four-quarter changes were right around 1.2 percent for core and 1 percent for headline.	13	1
201407	3238	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	As the Committee knows, in the first half of this year, headline PCE inflation has come up to about 1.8 percent, and core is about 1.6 percent.	13	1
201407	3239	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	And congratulations on your forecast.	13	1
201407	3240	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	In my view, the threat of inflation to remain persistently low has clearly diminished.	13	1
201407	3241	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	And there seems to be no threat of a breakout to the upside, given the apparent stability of long-run inflation expectations and the margins of slack that are likely to exist.	13	1
201407	3242	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Although the most recent readings for the second quarter are higher, a significant portion of movements in inflation from quarter to quarter, or even year to year, tends to be idiosyncratic and unrelated to fundamentals.	13	1
201407	3243	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Indeed, that’s one reason why the confidence intervals that David Wilcox addressed yesterday are as wide as they are.	13	1
201407	3244	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Also, looking at history and bearing in mind that the Committee does not do price-level targeting, it’s worth noting that the PCE price index level is about 3 percent lower than it would have been if inflation had been 2 percent since the end of 2007, and it’s 1½ percent lower than it would have been if inflation had run at 2 percent since the end of the recession in 2009.	13	1
201407	3245	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	So my bottom line, really, is that the evolution of inflation readings has been about what the Committee should want, and it doesn’t seem to me that inflation is currently sending a signal that policy is either too loose or too tight.	13	1
201407	3246	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	It actually seems to be about right.	13	1
201407	3247	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	With regard to labor markets, my view has been that significant slack remains.	13	1
201407	3248	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Payroll and employment data and other data for the first half of this year are sending a strong signal about improving health in the labor market.	13	1
201407	3249	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The level of slack in the labor market, in my view, has diminished by a substantial degree more than predicted by most forecasts, including even very recent forecasts.	13	1
201407	3250	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Still, the difference between headline unemployment and the estimated natural rate is something like ½ percent to 1 percentage point, according to the central tendency of the last SEP in June.	13	1
201407	3251	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Of course, the natural rate of unemployment is famously uncertain and subject to material revision, even years after the fact.	13	1
201407	3252	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	But there are other dimensions in which the labor market can adjust to a further strengthening in the economy.	13	1
201407	3253	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	In particular, the better-supported view, to me, is that the level of those working part time for economic reasons is materially higher than it should be, given the level of headline unemployment.	13	1
201407	3254	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	And even if most of the decline in the labor force participation rate is driven by demographics, there’s still almost certainly a cyclical component in that large decline since 2007.	13	1
201407	3255	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	So there are many outside the labor force who may come back in better conditions.	13	1
201407	3256	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Also, productivity has been low, and a tighter economy could produce higher productivity growth.	13	1
201407	3257	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Real wage growth has been well below what it should be, and a tighter economy could remedy that and pull even more back into the labor force.	13	1
201407	3258	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	My updated view is that, while slack is clearly diminishing, the labor market is not fully healed and significant slack remains.	13	1
201407	3259	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Now, if the July and August labor market readings validate or amplify this sense of the initial report for June, then I am prepared to revisit the pace of that healing and the desirable pace of removing monetary accommodation.	13	1
201407	3260	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	I have one final point on the baseline forecast.	13	1
201407	3261	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	I am not at all comfortable with the path described in the outyears, whereby the inertial Taylor (1999) rule is mechanically applied to drive output well above potential for several years in order to get inflation to go from 1¾ percent to 2 percent.	13	1
201407	3262	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	I wouldn’t do that, for two reasons:  first, the concern about leaning too hard on inflation expectations and, second, the concern about courting extreme financial market conditions with three or four more years of policy that is more accommodative than almost all of the standard policy rules.	13	1
201407	3263	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	As for implications for the current policy, there are none at this meeting, but I have to think about that.	13	1
201407	3264	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	These are more implications for the outyears than they are for today.	13	1
201407	3265	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Finally, in an effort to keep it interesting, I looked into a noteworthy development in the financial markets, which is that merger-and-acquisition activity has suddenly and significantly increased this year in the United States, Western Europe, and, to a lesser degree, Asia.	13	1
201407	3266	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	There are lots of different estimates, but a representative one suggests that, by dollar volume, announced M&A activity is up 70 percent this year in the Americas and 53 percent globally.	13	1
201407	3267	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The level of announced deals is on track to perhaps break the all-time record and beat the levels of 2006 and 2007.	13	1
201407	3268	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	M&A is often said to be a leading indicator of the “animal spirits” of business leaders, and I looked into it with a view to whether there are any implications for financial stability and the economy.	13	1
201407	3269	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	First, the character of the activity is very different from that in the pre-crisis era.	13	1
201407	3270	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	These are not the highly leveraged private equity transactions that were common in those years.	13	1
201407	3271	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Rather, these are transactions between large corporations heavily in telecom, media, technology, and health care.	13	1
201407	3272	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Some of these are the so-called tax-inversion mergers, which may have fiscal implications, but they wouldn’t be of a scope or level that would have macroeconomic implications, at least in the near term.	13	1
201407	3273	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Practitioners say that there’s a big wave of transactions coming in other industries as well.	13	1
201407	3274	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The prices that are being paid by strategic acquirers are substantially higher than private equity can pay or should pay, and that is the natural order of things.	13	1
201407	3275	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	And the fact that, before the crisis, private equity was winning auctions against corporates was a real sign of the pre-crisis leveraged-finance bubble.	13	1
201407	3276	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	But the memories of private-equity firms and debt investors of the big, bad deals of those years have apparently not faded yet.	13	1
201407	3277	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Practitioners also say that it is the extremely low cost of financing for investment-grade corporates, using either debt or highly valued stock, that generates unusually attractive returns for buyers, even at very high valuation levels.	13	1
201407	3278	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	One long-time basic stylized fact about M&A is that the buyer’s stock always declines on the announcement.	13	1
201407	3279	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	But in the first half of this year, in almost three-fourths of the cases, the buyer’s stock has gone up on the announcement.	13	1
201407	3280	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	This is unheard of, in my experience, and along the lines of seeing a snow leopard on Park Avenue in July.	13	1
201407	3281	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	[Laughter]	13	1
201407	3282	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The market is looking at these transactions as strategically well founded and adding value for shareholders, despite the high prices being paid.	13	1
201407	3283	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The interesting question, of course, is, what are the financial-stability and economic implications?	13	1
201407	3284	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	In my view, there really are no implications for financial stability.	13	1
201407	3285	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The investment-grade corporate sector is long on cash and low on leverage, so this is just very different from the pre-crisis years.	13	1
201407	3286	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The economic implications of this merger wave seem to me to be mixed.	13	1
201407	3287	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	The corporate model since the crisis has been to cut costs—margins are now and have been at all-time historic levels—and use the free cash flow to pay dividends and buy back stock, not to invest in facilities or hire employees.	13	1
201407	3288	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	So this sudden desire to merge is a new feature of the model.	13	1
201407	3289	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	I guess the positive slant would be to say that companies are willing to take risks again—the return of corporate animal spirits—and that could be a good thing.	13	1
201407	3290	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	It could lead to more hiring and more investment.	13	1
201407	3291	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	But in the near term, these transactions are not about investment, and they’re not about hiring.	13	1
201407	3292	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	They’re really about using the very low cost of capital to buy existing assets and about getting cost savings by reducing jobs, not about investment or hiring.	13	1
201407	3293	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	So I would say there’s a mixed picture on that.	13	1
201407	3294	2014-07-30	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201407	3298	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	After this very puzzling and seemingly anomalous first quarter, we are seeing data that provide a more consistent pattern pointing toward an acceleration of the pace at which slack is being eliminated.	13	1
201407	3299	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	Many labor market indicators, as many around the table have indicated, are finally improving at a faster pace than had been anticipated, and I’m particularly heartened by the increase in job openings, the reduction in long-term unemployment, and the more robust rate of quits.	13	1

201407	3322	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	With our wind-down of asset purchases now all but baked in the cake, our key decisions as we go forward pertain to the timing of liftoff and the pace at which the federal funds target range will rise thereafter.	13	1
201407	3323	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Our forward guidance specifies that these decisions should be data dependent, based, in particular, on realized progress, and the expected pace of future progress, toward the attainment of our maximum-employment and 2 percent inflation objectives.	13	1

201407	3332	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	For example, job openings have risen sharply.	13	1

201407	3334	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	The prevalence of hard-to-fill jobs is rising, and household surveys suggest some improvement in confidence in the labor market.	13	1

201407	3339	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	But confidence intervals around estimates of the natural rate of unemployment, as many of you noted, are very wide, and, conceivably, the unemployment gap may already have closed.	13	1
201407	3340	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	We continue to debate whether U-3 is a sufficient statistic for labor market slack.	13	1
201407	3341	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	A number of you commented that the decline we’ve seen in U-3 overstates the decline in overall labor market slack, reflecting an unusually large fraction of the workforce that’s involuntarily unemployed and an unusually depressed labor force participation rate.	13	1
201407	3342	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	In other words, shadow unemployment may be disproportionately high relative to U-3, but several of you also expressed skepticism about this argument.	13	1

201407	3344	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	In general, I think most of you see wage increases as remaining subdued and certainly below the benchmark of inflation plus productivity growth that would keep the labor share of income stable.	13	1

201407	3346	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	So we should probably not expect it to move back toward historical norms.	13	1
201407	3347	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	On the issue of the likely pace of future labor market gains, the Tealbook has edged up its projection of the pace of payroll employment growth, suggesting that the progress toward our goal may be quickening.	13	1
201407	3348	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Of course, it’s been surprising to see job creation running at such a fast pace in the face of such slow growth in output, but most of you seem to agree with the Tealbook’s assessment that the sharp decline in GDP in the first quarter was not reflective of the underlying strength of the expansion, even during the first quarter.	13	1
201407	3349	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	It appears to contain a large noise component, and today’s second-quarter GDP report and a range of indicators on spending, production, and business confidence, as well as the labor market data, support the expectation of stronger growth in the second half.	13	1

201407	3375	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	It pertains to the salaries of recent college graduates.	13	1

201407	3377	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Going back to the early 1990s, there is a clear pattern of recent college graduates’ earnings lagging those of all full-time workers in the aftermath of recessions.	13	1
201407	3378	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Strikingly, this same pattern has now persisted for six straight years, suggesting yet another dimension of the labor market that suggests the recovery remains far from complete.	13	1

201407	3533	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	The largest contributor to the variance of inflation—and I’m going to put this in very clinical terms—is what we antiseptically refer to as the idiosyncratic component, a physician would say “the idiopathic component.”	22	2
201407	3534	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	That is the part we can’t trace to any specific cause.	22	2
201407	3535	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	So random variation matters overwhelmingly for the determination of inflation.	22	2
201407	3536	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	As one of you pointed out yesterday, that’s an important piece of why the confidence intervals around inflation are so large.	22	2
201407	3537	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	The component that we discuss in future inflation determination that you have any leverage over, which is the degree of utilization of resources, explains really quite a small portion of the variance of future inflation.	22	2
201407	3538	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	So I want to be clear that there’s a lot of stuff that will push inflation around that we can’t foresee and over which you have quite little control.	22	2
201407	3539	2014-07-30	meeting	WILCOX	That is why there’s a very good probability that we will, in fact, attain the 2 percent objective without having to overheat the economy.	22	2

201407	3708	2014-07-30	meeting	PLOSSER	Indeed, it’s time that we abandon our current communications and forward guidance and instead work toward a better articulation of a reaction function.	23	2

201407	3713	2014-07-30	meeting	PLOSSER	In December 2013, which was the meeting at which we started to taper, my economic projections indicated that, by the fourth quarter of 2014, the unemployment rate would have declined to 6.2 percent and we would have headline PCE, measured from fourth quarter to fourth quarter, at 1.8 percent.	23	2
201407	3714	2014-07-30	meeting	PLOSSER	Consistent with that projection, I believe that an appropriate monetary policy would be a funds rate of about 1.25 percent by year-end.	23	2

201407	3716	2014-07-30	meeting	PLOSSER	The economy has now reached my December 2014 projections in terms of inflation and unemployment, nearly six months earlier than anticipated in December, and it’s projected to reach my December 2015 projections by the end of this year.	23	2
201407	3717	2014-07-30	meeting	PLOSSER	Now, you might say, well, that’s your problem—that’s not the Committee’s problem.	23	2
201407	3718	2014-07-30	meeting	PLOSSER	But I’d also note that if you go to the December 2013 SEP, we are very close to what our projections were for December 2015, 18 months ahead of where we thought we would be.	23	2

201407	4010	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I do agree with some of the comments from participants around the table that we will need to revisit the words “considerable time” in the very near term and try to provide more clarity about what exactly we mean by that.	23	2
201407	4011	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I would hope we do that in terms of economic conditions and not so much in terms of dates, but I think that, very soon, in the September– October time frame, we’re really going to be put in a position of having to try to answer some tough questions about that.	23	2
201407	4012	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	For a long period of time, this Committee has been struggling with the employment mandate and focused on the employment mandate as really the main challenge facing us.	23	2
201407	4013	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m increasingly thinking that the big challenge for us is really on price stability, and the perhaps overly extended dialogue that we had leading into this round highlights some of those challenges.	23	2
201407	4014	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Staff estimates right now are that inflation expectations are 1¾ percent.	23	2
201407	4015	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think that, if you carefully read the memo that we received in June, this estimate is unduly generous to this Committee.	23	2
201407	4016	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you actually read the numbers behind the assessment, you could have gone with a lower number, as Mr Wilcox mentioned earlier, in fact.	23	2
201407	4017	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We heard lots of passion yesterday about the loss of credibility associated with missing the federal funds rate target range by 10 basis points for one day.	23	2
201407	4018	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We need to bring that same level of passion, or even more, to the prospect of missing our inflation target by 25 basis points or more over an extended period.	23	2
201407	4019	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	When we have a flat Phillips curve, that miss of 25 basis points represents lots of resource loss and lots of underutilization.	23	2
201407	4020	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I’m concerned that FOMC participants seem comfortable with long-run misses of inflation.	23	2
201407	4021	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Maybe I’m misreading the conversation—I apologize if I am.	23	2
201407	4022	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I am concerned that I’m hearing a degree of sanguineness about having inflation underrun our target for a very long period of time.	23	2
201407	4023	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	President Evans asked me if I had some good points to make, and I said I didn’t, but I do have a lot of points.	23	2
201407	4024	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	[Laughter]	23	2
201407	4025	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	President Plosser, I think, raised a good point about how our behavior is likely to deviate from rules as we move forward.	23	2
201407	4026	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One reason for that lies in the behavior of inflation.	23	2
201407	4027	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Inflation has been so low for so long relative to target that we’re starting to see these estimates of what inflation expectations are drift downward.	23	2
201407	4028	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That really forces us to be more accommodative than we’d otherwise be, given the current metrics.	23	2
201407	4029	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As we contemplate how to interpret those words “considerable time,” my own thinking about how we should be formulating the conditions governing liftoff is really in terms of the inflation mandate and the price stability mandate.	23	2
201407	4030	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s very hard to justify liftoff taking place when the one- to two-year-ahead outlook for inflation is below 2 percent.	23	2
201407	4031	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In my mind, there’s no reason for us to be lowering rates simply because unemployment has gotten too low.	23	2
201407	4032	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s built into Tealbook objectives—that there’s a loss associated with unemployment being too low.	23	2
201407	4033	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t read our statutory mandate as saying that you should not have unemployment be too low.	23	2
201407	4034	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t read our communications from our long-run goals statement as saying, “We don’t want unemployment to be too low.”	23	2
201407	4035	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I would not be supportive of raising rates simply because unemployment has fallen too low.	23	2
201407	4036	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The other piece that’s built into Tealbook objectives is a desire to keep interest rates smooth.	23	2
201407	4037	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In fact, as the Tealbook, Book B, notes, a lot of weight is put on that.	23	2
201407	4038	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t see that in our statutory mandates.	23	2
201407	4039	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t see it in our long-run goals or communications.	23	2
201407	4040	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	For those reasons, I don’t put weight on that in my objective.	23	2
201407	4041	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Accordingly, I would see no reason for us to be raising rates as long as the inflation outlook remained below 2 percent.	23	2
201407	4042	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I say “no reason.”	23	2
201407	4043	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There is one reason—potential financial instability concerns, which I’ve talked about in the past.	23	2
201407	4044	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I agree with Governor Fischer here.	23	2
201407	4045	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As long as housing is not being affected by this, it’s really hard for me to see how this is going to get into the broader economy.	23	2
201407	4046	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s where you’re going to see it—in housing.	23	2
201407	4047	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Until we see it there—and we’re a long way from that, unfortunately—I don’t think that’s going to be a risk.	23	2
201407	4048	2014-07-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	When I think about how to reframe “considerable time,” it would be to frame it in terms of the necessary condition for liftoff being a one- to two-year-ahead outlook for inflation of 2 percent.	23	2

201407	4164	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	That growing divergence between market expectations and our internal projections argues in favor of altering the description of economic conditions, as is reflected in the proposed alternative B statement.	23	2
201407	4165	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	I would hope the market would have put greater weight, actually, on the data relative to the forward guidance, based on the statement made by the Chair in her Humphrey-Hawkins testimony.	23	2
201407	4166	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	Here I’m referring to the statement that “if the labor market continues to improve more quickly than anticipated ...	23	2
201407	4167	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	That does not seem to have gotten much traction.	23	2
201407	4168	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	And I would think we would want to see that sentiment, the data dependency, start to dominate the market’s thinking.	23	2
201407	4169	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	It’s worth thinking a little bit about the tradeoff between waiting a little longer with a possibly steeper path of rates thereafter, versus moving a little sooner and thinking that we could actually see a somewhat shallower path.	23	2
201407	4170	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	If the dominant consideration is not moving prematurely in the context of considerable uncertainty about the extent of slack, then the later time of liftoff and possibly steeper path thereafter probably are preferable.	23	2
201407	4171	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	If, instead, we were particularly worried about risks to financial stability, then perhaps we would be more inclined toward the sooner, shallower path.	23	2
201407	4172	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	It’s helpful, as many people have done here, to discipline our discussions about this thinking forward to a sequence of signals that we would be sending over the next few meetings as more data come in—especially, again, because it’s going to be very difficult to reverse course once that alteration is made.	23	2
201407	4173	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	So I think we would want to tilt toward a more positive read on the elimination of slack and inflation in this statement.	23	2
201407	4174	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	In September, we’ll have the new SEP numbers, which will move considerably—if I hear people around the table correctly—and the market will see that.	23	2
201407	4175	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	The Chair will be giving a press conference, and we’ll also have the principles on exit.	23	2
201407	4176	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	Those things together will give us the potential for adjustments based on the data that we’ll have in the intervening period.	23	2
201407	4177	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	Thereafter, if, in fact, we continue to see this acceleration of the elimination of slack, I do think we have to start moving away from the “considerable time” language and altering the forward guidance.	23	2
201407	4178	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	With regard to the actual characterization of underutilization, I have to say I share the concerns that have been articulated that the characterization of slack as “significant underutilization” does seem a bit strong in relation to the data that we’ve seen.	23	2
201407	4179	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	The real concern is, how do you move off of that without sending too strong a signal?	23	2
201407	4180	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	I think Governor Tarullo has suggested two alternatives, and President Mester stated that tradeoff nicely.	23	2
201407	4181	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	Again, I would defer to the Chair, but that is something that I would give a little bit more thought to.	23	2
201407	4182	2014-07-30	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you.	23	2
201407	4183	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	23	2
201407	4184	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Vice Chairman.	23	2
201407	4185	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201407	4186	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I support alternative B as written.	23	2
201407	4187	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But before I get into the language, I’d like to make some observations about the outlook for monetary policy over the longer term.	23	2
201407	4188	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	It strikes me that the prospects for an earlier-than-anticipated tightening of monetary policy have increased.	23	2
201407	4189	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I, for one, can certainly imagine liftoff occurring next March, presuming that the outlook evolves in line with the Tealbook forecast or something close to that.	23	2
201407	4190	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Given that, we need to start thinking about working backward.	23	2
201407	4191	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	How would we actually manage to a March liftoff?	23	2
201407	4192	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s not to say we absolutely will do that, but the data certainly could evolve in that way.	23	2
201407	4193	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The way I see it happening is in a number of steps.	23	2
201407	4194	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The first step happens today, when we basically move it a baby step, as Governor Tarullo said, just to start to change market expectations a little bit.	23	2
201407	4195	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	In September, if the next two employment reports confirm that we’re still on this trajectory, then we take a bigger step in terms of language.	23	2
201407	4196	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	And then in December, we remove the “considerable time” language, and that’s the signal that, at the next press conference meeting three months later, it’s highly likely that you’re actually going to go ahead with tightening.	23	2
201407	4197	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s how I think it would go.	23	2
201407	4198	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But we need to start to have some discussion around the table about how this sequence would work and get people in agreement on how this process would unfold.	23	2
201407	4199	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, the second question I want to talk about is something that Governor Fischer raised, which I think is interesting—this gap right now between what we think and what market expectations are.	23	2
201407	4200	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The question I want to pose is, how big a problem is this, and what should we do about it at this meeting?	23	2
201407	4201	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	My own view is, I’m probably a little bit less concerned about this problem than maybe some others at this meeting, and the reason for that is, consider two alternatives.	23	2
201407	4202	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The first alternative is a circumstance in which market participants expect us to go earlier than we think we’re going to go.	23	2
201407	4203	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The market has the tightening as occurring likely before we actually think we’re going to move.	23	2
201407	4204	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s a particularly dangerous circumstance because, if we don’t go when the market expects us to go, there’s a real risk of a loss of credibility and an unhinging of inflation expectations, and that could be very damaging.	23	2
201407	4205	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But that’s not the situation that we’re in.	23	2
201407	4206	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	We’re in the opposite situation, in which we think we might go earlier than the market thinks, and that’s less risky in the sense that there is less risk of it leading to a real loss of credibility for the FOMC in terms of its commitment to keeping inflation in check.	23	2
201407	4207	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Yes, there would be a credibility hit in the sense that we misled the markets, and, certainly, there would be some jolt to financial asset valuation.	23	2
201407	4208	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But at least the misalignment we have today is the less dangerous of the misalignments that can occur when market expectations and what we think are not lined up.	23	2
201407	4209	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, I think it’s important to dig a little deeper about why market participants are in a different place than we are.	23	2
201407	4210	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Is it because they don’t understand our reaction function, or is it because they just have a different forecast in terms of how the economy will evolve?	23	2
201407	4211	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	It’s interesting that, when you look at the Tealbook unemployment-rate projection and you compare that with the primary dealer survey unemployment-rate projection, you see a pretty wide gap.	23	2
201407	4212	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	In 2016, the Tealbook is at 4.7 percent, and market expectations are at 5.4 percent.	23	2
201407	4213	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Well, if that’s the reason for the disagreement about the timing of liftoff and the trajectory of interest rates, that’s not really something that we should be that concerned about, because one of those forecasts is going to be more right than the other and that gap will tend to close on its own as it becomes more clear whether the Tealbook forecast is the right forecast or the primary dealer forecast is the right forecast.	23	2
201407	4214	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So it’s really important to think about, what’s the reason for the disagreement?	23	2
201407	4215	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Is it a disagreement about the trajectory of the economy, or is it a disagreement due to a lack of understanding of our reaction function?	23	2
201407	4216	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If it’s a lack of understanding of our reaction function, that’s a much more serious problem.	23	2
201407	4217	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	In terms of alternative B, I do think it does exactly what we want to accomplish at this meeting.	23	2
201407	4218	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	It acknowledges the improvement in the labor market, it acknowledges the fact that the risks of inflation getting stuck far below our 2 percent objective have lessened, and those are the changes made in paragraphs 1 and 2.	23	2
201407	4219	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But it doesn’t jump yet to drawing implications for those changes in terms of what’s actually going to happen in the later paragraphs with respect to monetary policy.	23	2
201407	4220	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	It says we acknowledge what’s happened.	23	2
201407	4221	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	We’re on watch, but we’re not quite ready to actually put that into action in terms of monetary policy, and I think that’s about right.	23	2
201407	4222	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If the next two employment reports and the data are consistent with 3 percent growth and 200-thousand-plus payroll gains in the two months, then, in September, you’d want to start to make changes to those latter paragraphs about the monetary policy pieces.	23	2
201407	4223	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That would be the second step that would affect the change in market expectations that’s appropriate.	23	2
201407	4224	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	There will be a market reaction to the statement today.	23	2
201407	4225	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I think they will take it as our being more optimistic about the outlook and less concerned about inflation.	23	2
201407	4226	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So I believe that the market will take some signal from this.	23	2
201407	4227	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But the fact that we’re not changing the later paragraphs means the market reaction will probably be pretty mild.	23	2
201407	4228	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	In terms of the language issues, the big debate around the table seems to be about the word “significant” in paragraph 1.	23	2
201407	4229	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	My own view is that that word should stay in, and I think it should stay in for a couple of reasons.	23	2
201407	4230	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The first reason it should stay in is that that’s the language that the Chair used in her testimony.	23	2
201407	4231	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So people are going to be comparing the two, and, if it comes out, people are going to view that as quite meaningful.	23	2
201407	4232	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The second reason it should stay in is, it’s actually accurate.	23	2
201407	4233	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If you read not just that sentence, but also the changes that are made to paragraph 1 in its entirety, we’re basically saying that the unemployment rate is no longer elevated, in our view.	23	2
201407	4234	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	We’re acknowledging that it’s declined further, and we’re then qualifying that slightly by saying that, despite those things, which are good developments, we still think there’s “significant underutilization of labor resources.”	23	2
201407	4235	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If you read the statement as, we’re just saying there’s “significant underutilization of labor resources,” and that was the main point of the paragraph, then it could be up for debate.	23	2
201407	4236	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But the reality is, we’re using that sentence to qualify the other changes that we’ve made in that first paragraph.	23	2
201407	4237	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, to me, “significant” means meaningful, and the question I would have on the table is, do we think that the underutilization of labor resources that we have today is meaningful?	23	2
201407	4238	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Well, we almost certainly think that, because, if we didn’t think that, we’d probably be lifting off already.	23	2
201407	4239	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So it seems to me that “significant underutilization of labor resources” is exactly, completely paired with the policy that we’re actually pursuing.	23	2
201407	4240	2014-07-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you.	23	2

201407	4244	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	I believe everybody recognizes that we’re on a path here where, if the data continue to come in strong and to create a series of positive surprises, we’re likely going to be moving up sooner than we previously anticipated, and that we will have to signal our intention to do that in a series of steps.	23	2
201407	4245	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	I am more than prepared to go in that direction if the data continue as they have been.	23	2
201407	4246	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	So, to my mind, the questions with respect to language concern the sequence of steps that so many of you have talked about and what’s the right move for today.	23	2
201407	4247	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	It’s always hard to know what the market reaction is going to be to language.	23	2
201407	4248	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	I think, as many of you have indicated, the change in the characterization of the inflation outlook is going to be noted quite carefully and taken as a signal of a somewhat “hawkier” stance.	23	2
201407	4249	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	I don’t know what markets will think of the word “significant,” but my own interpretation was that it was exactly in line with what the Vice Chairman indicated—that it means “appreciable” or “meaningful,” and that the overall thrust of that paragraph is, we’re no longer saying the unemployment rate is elevated.	23	2
201407	4250	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	We’re saying that not only has the unemployment rate declined, but we think labor market indicators quite generally show that there’s also been improvement in the labor market, and we’ve moved away from “elevated.”	23	2
201407	4251	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	But I do think it’s accurate to say that the degree of underutilization is significant.	23	2
201407	4252	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Obviously, I’ve heard many of you who would be inclined to change the word “significant.”	23	2
201407	4253	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Also, I’ve heard a considerable number of you who think that it’s the right word for today.	23	2
201407	4254	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	My preference would be to stick with “significant,” and I suppose this point that this is exactly the language that, just over a week ago, I uttered in testimony and was in our Monetary Policy Report inclines me to keep it.	23	2
201407	4255	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Because of how markets interpret these things, to remove that word would be taken as a serious signal.	23	2
201407	4256	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	But September—obviously, we will have to reconsider that characterization of the labor market if we continue to see very strong labor reports.	23	2
201407	4257	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	And I agree with those of you who have talked about the forward guidance and the eventual need for that to change.	23	2
201407	4258	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	I think March is very much on the table as a time when we might begin to tighten.	23	2
201407	4259	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	I am concerned about the gap between market expectations and, perhaps, our own evolving expectations.	23	2
201407	4260	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Obviously, the SEP will show, if things continue this way, meaningful changes in September, and I am completely open to changing the language.	23	2
201407	4261	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	But I would like to do so in as smooth a way as possible and in a series of steps, if we can.	23	2
201407	4262	2014-07-30	meeting	YELLEN	Hearing the concerns that you have about where we are and where we need to go, I take those very seriously, and I’m ready to work with the Committee to move faster if the data call for that.	23	2
